    [Original] New Silent Hill game inspired by tiny fishing village in Fife
    Silent Hill: Townfall, the latest entry in the globally renowned survival horror franchise, is set in the fictional village of St Amelia, inspired by the real-life fishing community of St Monans in Fife, Scotland. Developed by Glasgow-based Screen Burn, a studio of around 30 employees, the game marks the companyâ€™s â€œbiggest and most ambitious project yet.â€ The franchise, originally launched by Japanese multinational Konami in 1999, has sold millions of units worldwide, with the most recent spin-off, Silent Hill f, selling a million copies on its September 2025 release day. Townfall, set for release later this year, will digitally populate St Ameliaâ€”home to just over 1,100 people in realityâ€”with potentially one million virtual visitors. A newly released trailer showcases the villageâ€™s haunting atmosphere, featuring a harbour lined with lobster boxes, bobbing boats, and vibrant bunting along fog-drenched streets. Placards near a monument read â€œlet the ferries inâ€ and â€œthis town is ours,â€ underscoring the eerie emptiness of the setting. Screen Burn co-founder Jon McKellan explained the team initially considered a vague northern European location but ultimately chose St Monans for authenticity. â€œThere was a drive to create something that felt authentic to us and it kind of felt natural to bring it home,â€ he said. The gameâ€™s narrative centers on isolation and existential dread, core themes in the series, with McKellan noting the townâ€™s sense of being trapped mirroring the franchiseâ€™s signature psychological tension. Recreating the local haarâ€”cold sea fog common along Scotlandâ€™s east coastâ€”was a key design focus. Art director Paul Abbott, who grew up in a small fishing village, described the misty environment as â€œbeautifully epic, grey, drizzly,â€ and emphasized the teamâ€™s meticulous attention to detail, including the winding alleyways and textured piers. McKellan called the collaboration with Konami â€œa genuine honour,â€ adding that the opportunity was one the team â€œknew we all wanted right away.â€ The game aims to blend the emotional resonance of real-world Scottish coastal life with the psychological horror that defines the Silent Hill legacy.

    [Original] Google puts users at risk by downplaying health disclaimers under AI Overviews
    Google is exposing users to potential harm by failing to display prominent safety warnings when first presenting AI-generated medical advice through its AI Overviews, according to a Guardian investigation. While Google claims its AI Overviews encourage users to seek professional medical advice and include disclaimers when appropriate, the company does not display any warnings at the initial stage of a health-related query. Instead, disclaimers only appear after users click â€œShow moreâ€ to access additional AI-generated information, and even then, they are placed at the bottom of the summary in a smaller, lighter font. The disclaimer reads: â€œThis is for informational purposes only. For medical advice or a diagnosis, consult a professional. AI responses may include mistakes.â€ Experts and patient advocates have criticized this approach. Pat Pataranutaporn, an MIT researcher specializing in AI and human-computer interaction, warned that AI models can hallucinate misinformation or prioritize user satisfaction over accuracy, especially in healthcare contexts. Gina Neff, a professor of responsible AI at Queen Mary University of London, stated the problem is by designâ€”AI Overviews prioritize speed over accuracy, increasing the risk of dangerous errors. She emphasized that requiring users to click through to find disclaimers creates a significant risk, as many may not read beyond the initial summary. Sonali Sharma, a Stanford AIMI researcher, noted that AI Overviews appear at the top of search results and often give the impression of a complete, authoritative answer. This can discourage users from further research or verifying information, especially when the content contains partially correct and partially incorrect details. Tom Bishop, head of patient information at Anthony Nolan, called for immediate action, urging Google to place disclaimers at the top of the page in a clear, prominent font. â€œIâ€™d like this disclaimer to be right at the top. Iâ€™d like it to be the first thing you see,â€ he said, stressing that users must be prompted to question whether they should act on the information or consult a medical professional. Google maintains that AI Overviews frequently mention seeking medical attention within the summary and that disclaimers are present, but critics argue the current implementation fails to protect users effectively.

    [Original] Why did I get a Â£100 parking fine when charging my electric car?
    Anna Tims received a Â£100 parking charge notice (PCN) from Ocean Parking for using a â€œ24-hourâ€ Mer EV charger at a B&Q car park in Redhill, despite the chargerâ€™s website advertising round-the-clock availability. Ocean cited a restriction prohibiting parking between 9pm and 6am, which was not indicated on the EV charger sign or entrance signage. The only reference to terms and conditions appeared in small print beneath a â€œfree parking up to two hoursâ€ sign, with no mention of restrictions at the charging point itself. Mer EV acknowledged the confusion caused by its website claim and has since updated it. Ocean initially denied liability, stating it was unaware the charger was operational until Tims raised the issue, and maintained that EV users were not exempt from parking rules. However, after criticism, Ocean admitted signage at the charging area met only minimum standards and, despite its reputation for exceeding them, issued a pragmatic reversal: all outstanding PCNs at the site were cancelled, appeals upheld, and refunds offered to those who had paid. In a separate case, CH was issued a Â£100 PCN by Euro Parking Services (EPS) for parking on â€œdouble yellow lines/crosshatched bayâ€ in a â€œrestricted areaâ€ in a Leicestershire business park while drinking coffee. EPS required photographic evidence of the sign, prompting CH to return and take photos of a tiny, high-mounted sign with minuscule printâ€”visible only from a distance. CCTV footage showed no yellow lines or hatchings on the road. EPS, a member of the International Parking Community (IPC), was found to violate its own code, which mandates conspicuous, legible signage and a five-minute grace period. Despite this, EPS upheld the first PCN, citing CHâ€™s 75-second stay, and issued a second Â£100 charge after he photographed the sign. EPS later withdrew the second charge, stating it recognizes drivers must be able to read and photograph signs to appeal, but insisted on keeping the first. CH can appeal to the IPC, though losing would forfeit a Â£40 early payment discount. Both cases highlight widespread issues with unclear signage and inconsistent enforcement of parking rules, particularly in EV charging areas.

    [Original] â€˜This shouldnâ€™t be normalâ€™: developers speak out about bigotry on Steam, the worldâ€™s biggest PC gaming storefront
    Steam, the worldâ€™s largest PC gaming storefront, faces mounting criticism over its failure to enforce community guidelines, with developers reporting rampant bigotry, harassment, and coordinated campaigns targeting inclusive or LGBTQ+ content. Multiple creators, including transgender and non-binary developers, describe a platform where abusive reviews, discriminatory remarks, and targeted campaigns persist unchecked. Nathalie Lawhead, developer of *Tetrageddon* and *Blue Suburbia*, spent two years attempting to remove reviews calling her a â€œliarâ€ and making antisemitic remarks, including one stating she â€œprobably has dual Israeli citizenship with how pointy her nose is.â€ Despite reporting these posts, Steam initially cleared them, citing that it does not verify the accuracy of user reviews and avoids moderation to prevent accusations of censorship. Lawhead later succeeded in getting the antisemitic review removed only after enlisting public support and personally contacting a Valve employeeâ€”highlighting what she calls a â€œbrokenâ€ moderation system. Other developers report similar experiences. Ethan, creator of *Coven*, was targeted by â€œCharlieTweetsDetected,â€ a curator promoting games based on perceived political alignment, particularly around the assassination of rightwing activist Charlie Kirk. Reviews referencing Kirk, including â€œRIP Charlie Kirk,â€ remain on the platform despite being off-topic, with Steam support dismissing them as not violating guidelines. A â€œNO WOKEâ€ curation list has amplified such attacks, fostering hostile discussion threads. Valve, which employs fewer than 400 people, handles hundreds of thousands of support tickets weekly, yet provides no public explanation for its moderation decisions. Former volunteer moderators and developers alike suggest the system is outsourced and ineffective. Responses from Steam support, including one to Lawhead in 2026 and another to developer Phi in 2021, repeatedly emphasize a â€œyes/noâ€ decision-making model, advising creators to â€œwork on the productâ€ and rely on community upvoting instead of moderation. This approach leaves hate speech and targeted harassment unaddressed. Developers like Phi and Mike Rose of No More Robots have resorted to public pushback, while others, like Ambrosia Skyâ€™s Christina Pollock, now repurpose derogatory comments into marketing copy. With nearly 42 million concurrent users and unmatched industry clout, Steam remains essential for developersâ€”despite its failure to protect marginalized creators or maintain a safe environment.

    [Original] User Issues With X Appear to Have Resolved
    Users reported issues with websites and apps, including X, early Monday, according to the online outage tracker DownDetector, but most problems appeared to have resolved by midmorning. It was not immediately clear on Monday morning what caused the disruption for X. Reports of problems began shortly after 8 a.m. Eastern, according to DownDetector, and appeared to be mostly resolved by 10 a.m. Earlier in the morning, Cloudflare, a tech company that provides a wide range of services for apps and websites, said it was experiencing a minor issue but that it did not appear to be affecting its services for clients. A spokeswoman for the company said there had been no outages on Monday and that the issues were unrelated. An earlier version of this article referred imprecisely to the issues Cloudflare reported. It said it had detected an issue potentially affecting its services; it did not report any outage. When we learn of a mistake, we acknowledge it with a correction.     [Original] Six of Sarah Ferguson's companies winding down
    ShareSaveLucy HookerBusiness reporterShareSaveGetty ImagesSix companies linked to the former Duchess of York, Sarah Ferguson, are being wound down, documents filed with Companies House show. Ferguson is the director of all six businesses that are due to be struck off the register in ten days - unless legal cause is shown to prevent it. It is unclear what the firms' activities were, although one is related to public relations and another to retail. None appear to be very commercially active. Their closure comes in the wake of further revelations from the Epstein files which suggest the ex-wife of Andrew Mountbatten-Windsor told the wealthy paedophile how desperate she felt over her financial difficulties. Email exchanges between Ferguson and Epstein indicate she asked for his financial advice over how to deal with her Â£6m debt pile while he was still in jail for soliciting prostitution from a minor. Being named among the Epstein files is not an indication of wrongdoing. A spokesperson for the former Duchess of York has been approached for comment. The companies that are set to close are S Phoenix Events, Fergie's Farm, La Luna Investments, Solamoon Ltd, Philanthrapreneur Ltd and Planet Partners Productions Ltd, none of which have any public profile. Listings for five of the six companies state there is an "application to strike the company off the register". That means the company will stop trading and will cease to exist. The sixth, S Pheonix Events, which also notes the receipt of an application to strike off, lists Sarah Ferguson as the person authenticating the application on behalf of the company. Ferguson is listed as an active director for three other businesses registered with Companies House: Ginger and Moss, set up as a lifestyle brand to sell tea, jewellery and housewares, a "motion picture production activities" business called Coat, and Librasol, classified under "artistic creation" on the official register for private companies. All the companies being dissolved were set up more than a decade ago. Most are classed as "dormant companies", with very little or no reported recent commercial activity. Ferguson is the sole remaining director for all of them. Late last year, documents show she submitted an official change to her details for all the firms from Sarah Duchess of York to Sarah Margaret Ferguson. There have been reports of Ferguson's financial difficulties for many years, but the publication of the files of Jeffrey Epstein's correspondence has laid bare how much pressure she was under. One of the files suggests she told Epstein she was about to "freak with exhaustion" at a time when he was still in custody in Florida and that she also leaned on him for emotional support.

    [Original] Royal Mail letters sit undelivered 'for weeks' as parcels prioritised, staff say
    More than a dozen Royal Mail postal workers across the UK have reported widespread delivery failures, with letters frequently left undelivered for weeks while parcels are prioritized, according to BBC Your Voice. Workers describe a system stretched beyond capacity, with full mail racks left untouched daily and first-class letters sitting for up to two weeks. One postman, Tony (not his real name), shared photos of stacked mail, including a tray of first-class letters that had been unattended for two weeks. He confirmed parcelsâ€”especially tracked onesâ€”are consistently prioritized, even over first-class mail, due to financial incentives and space constraints. Another worker, Bob, said that since January, overtime has been nearly eliminated, and multiple delivery rounds are skipped, particularly during staff annual leave. â€œEvery day thereâ€™s mail left behind,â€ Bob said, noting that only tracked parcels are delivered daily, while untracked mail is often delayed indefinitely. The company acknowledged service disruptions affecting 105 postcodes across 38 delivery offices, citing storms in Januaryâ€”Goretti, Ingrid, and Chandraâ€”and higher staff sickness rates. Royal Mail stated that the vast majority of mail is delivered as planned and that performance is monitored daily. However, staff dispute this, saying that during the Christmas period, some offices did not sort mail for two days. The Communication Workers Union (CWU) now describes Royal Mail as â€œa company in crisis,â€ citing a lack of confidence in promised improvements since the companyâ€™s acquisition by the Czech ED Group last April. Ofcom has previously fined Royal Mail Â£37 million for poor letter delivery performance and pledged continued oversight. Despite the challenges, some customers report consistent service. Clive Miller from Oxford said his first-class letters have always arrived on time. Royal Mail maintains that it aims to deliver both letters and parcels on time, noting that parcels take up more space and can create health risks if piled up. The company says it deploys extra support to resolve delays and reviews performance daily. However, frontline staff remain skeptical, citing systemic under-resourcing and prioritization of parcels over letters.

    [Original] Lloyds boss accepts concern over use of staff data in pay talks
    ShareSaveArchie MitchellBusiness reporterShareSavePA MediaCharlie Nunn said the revelation had obviously "created some concern".The chief executive of Lloyds Banking Group has acknowledged there have been concerns over its use of information from staff bank accounts as part of pay negotiations. Charlie Nunn said "we have definitely listened" after the UK's biggest lender was criticised for comparing employees' spending habits to the wider public to show if they weathered the cost-of-living crisis better. The group was found to have looked at the data of staff who banked with Lloyds, along with the saving rates and salary increases of its lowest paid workers, and assessed them against its customers. In a recent town hall meeting with staff, Nunn said the revelation "obviously has created some concern". In comments first reported by The Times, he added: "We haven't yet fully worked out what we will do differently going forward, although I think we should just do the investigation fully." During the same event, the Lloyds chief made clear to staff that he stood by the approach taken by the bank. Lloyds said there is no formal investigation into the use of staff account data, but that bosses were reflecting on the tactics ahead of next year's pay talks. The bank faced questions from information watchdog the Information Commissioner's Office (ICO) over the episode, but it has not faced a formal ICO probe. Lloyds used what it described as "aggregated, anonymised data" to "ensure compliance with regulations and to reflect common practice of using data to underpin decision-making". In a presentation, it told unions its workers' finances fared better than those of the wider public in recent years. Employees are actively encouraged to bank with Lloyds and sign up for accounts as a condition of employment. The pay talks saw junior colleagues eventually offered increases of between 7% and 9%, with salaries jumping by Â£1,200 this year and next, to a minimum of Â£27,400. Unions recognised by the bank welcomed the pay agreement, with Accord general secretary Get Nichols saying at the time the analysis was "really helpful". But Mark Brown, general secretary of the Affinity union, which represents Lloyds' employees but is not recognised by the group, said the bank had "no legitimate reason" to access staff accounts. On Monday, a Lloyds spokesperson said: "Lloyds Banking Group is committed to fair and progressive pay that provides certainty and support for all colleagues, and in this case more junior colleagues. "We have worked hard with our unions, using aggregated data and direct colleague input and we are pleased that members of our recognised unions have voted to support our competitive multi-year pay proposal for 2026 and 2027 by a significant majority."

    [Original] Brewdog staff 'upset and concerned' by sale plans
    ShareSaveShareSavePA MediaBrewdog had called in financial experts to attract new investorsBrewdog employees are "really concerned" for their futures having learned the craft beer giant could be sold off, a union has said. Financial experts AlixPartners have been brought in to attract new investors in a move which would potentially see the Aberdeenshire-based firm broken up. The Unite union said "upset" staff had only received a single email from senior management - with some only finding out about the potential sale after it was reported in the press. In a separate email to staff, seen by BBC Scotland News, Brewdog said the decision was a "normal and prudent step". The company said it "did not change day-to-day operations, our roles or our immediate plans". In a media statement, the company said it had taken the decision to bring in AlixPartners after "operating in a challenging economic climate". Brewdog said it wanted to focus on the "long-term strength and sustainably" of the company. Bryan Simpson, hospitality lead for Unite, said the union had been "inundated" with messages from staff worried about their livelihoods since the news emerged. He said some workers had already had their hours cut from 32 to 24 per week, meaning they were about Â£400 worse off. The company currently employs around 1,400 people and has breweries in Ellon as well as in the US, Australia and Germany. Simpson said many of those contacting the union for advice were employed at the Ellon site. He said: "Let's be clear, this isn't just the potential collapse of a brand, this is people's jobs, this is people's rent, how they pay their bills and their childcare and yet they are being informed about the sale of their employer through the press. That is morally unacceptable. "We need clarity on jobs. If there are going to be redundancies, they need to be informed as soon as possible, legally and morally, because they cannot have job losses with consultation and without ensuring they get the maximum they are due." Brewdog was founded by friends James Watt and Martin Dickie in 2007 and now has bars and breweries around the world, including about 60 in the UK. The company sold a 22.3% stake to San Francisco-based private equity firm TSG Consumer Partners in 2017. Watt then stood down as CEO in 2024 and moved to a new position as "co-founder and captain". Dickie left the company last year for personal reasons. In January, the company said it would stop making its Lonewolf Gin and Abstrakt Vodka brands at its distillery in Ellon. That came a few months after job cuts were announced across the business after it posted a Â£37m loss. In recent years it has made headlines for its marketing campaigns and workplace culture. Brewdog faced backlash in 2024 after revealing it would no longer hire new staff on the real living wage, instead paying the lower legal minimum wage.

    [Original] ByteDance to curb AI video app after Disney legal threat
    Chinese tech giant ByteDance has pledged to strengthen safeguards on its AI video tool, Seedance, following legal threats from Disney and other entertainment companies over alleged copyright infringement. The controversy centers on Seedance 2.0, which launched on February 12 and has generated viral videos featuring realistic depictions of characters from Marvel, Star Wars, and other franchises. Disney sent a cease-and-desist letter accusing ByteDance of supplying the platform with a "pirated library" of copyrighted material, including superheroes and cartoon characters, calling the actions a "virtual smash-and-grab" of intellectual property. The company also cited concerns over the unauthorised use of likenesses of real actors and characters from its franchises, including those from Pixar, Marvel, and Star Wars. In response, ByteDance told the BBC it "respects intellectual property rights" and acknowledged concerns about Seedance 2.0, stating it is taking steps to prevent unauthorised use of IP. However, the company did not disclose specific measures. Seedance, like other generative AI tools, creates videos from text prompts and has been used to generate clips showing Anakin Skywalker and Rey battling with lightsabres, and Spider-Man fighting Captain America in New York City. The company has not revealed the data used to train the model. Paramount Skydance has reportedly sent a similar cease-and-desist letter, while the Motion Picture Association, representing studios including Warner Bros Discovery, Paramount, and Netflix, has demanded the tool "immediately cease its infringing activity." The actorsâ€™ union Sag-Aftra has also accused Seedance of "blatant infringement." Japanâ€™s government has launched an investigation into ByteDance over AI-generated anime content, raising further copyright concerns. This follows previous legal actions, including Disney and NBCUniversalâ€™s lawsuit against AI image generator Midjourney, which alleges the platform created "endless unauthorised copies" of copyrighted worksâ€”an ongoing case. Disney has also urged Google to restrict the generation of its characters on Googleâ€™s AI platforms.

    [Original] Trump eyes Venezuela visit â€“ but obstacles to his oil plan remain
    Venezuela possesses the worldâ€™s largest oil reserves, officially estimated at 300 billion barrels, yet in 2023 exported only 211.6 million barrels worth $4 billionâ€”far below its potential. Following U.S. President Donald Trumpâ€™s recent vow to exploit these reserves and his reported plans to visit the country, U.S. Energy Secretary Chris Wright completed a two-day trip to assess the feasibility of reopening Venezuelaâ€™s oil sector to American firms. The move follows Venezuelaâ€™s National Assembly passing a law enabling private and foreign investment after two decades of state control under Maduro and ChÃ¡vez. Trump has urged U.S. oil companies to invest at least $100 billion to rebuild the sector, claiming it could dramatically increase supply and lower consumer prices. However, experts question the economic viability. PDVSA, the state-owned oil company, is severely degraded due to years of neglect and mismanagement, with production down by 1.5 million barrels per day compared to a decade ago. Equipment is outdated, and skilled personnel have fled, with nearly eight million Venezuelans emigrating since the economic collapse. Monica de Bolle of the Peterson Institute warns that PDVSA is a nationalist symbol, making foreign control politically untenable. Analysts also cast doubt on the true size of Venezuelaâ€™s reserves, noting a dramatic increase during ChÃ¡vezâ€™s tenure tied to high oil prices, which may no longer justify the figures. Additionally, Venezuelaâ€™s heavy, sour crude is harder and more corrosive to refine than Saudi oil, posing technical challenges. While Canadian oil remains competitive even if Venezuelan output rises, U.S. firms remain hesitant. ExxonMobil CEO Darren Woods has labeled Venezuela â€œuninvestable,â€ citing past expropriationsâ€”such as the 2007 seizure of assets from ExxonMobil and ConocoPhillips, which were awarded $8.3 billion in damages, never paid. The Trump administration has offered no security guarantees, and Wright confirmed no such assurances are planned, heightening risks. De Bolle criticizes the policy as â€œall stick, no carrot,â€ reflecting an â€œimperialist visionâ€ of Latin America. Without incentives, private firms are unlikely to invest. As Jackson of Capital Economics notes, â€œYou can build anything, as long as you can pay for it,â€ but current oil prices around $65 per barrel make large-scale investment unattractive. The outcome remains uncertain, with analysts stressing the geopolitical complexity and opacity of the situation.

    [Original] Bedding firm backs down on 'Swift Home' trademark after Taylor Swift appeal
    ShareSaveOsmond ChiaBusiness reporterShareSavePAA firm behind a bid to trademark the brand "Swift Home" for a line of bedding products has withdrawn its application over the weekend, following an appeal by pop star Taylor Swift to the US patents office. A representative for Cathay Home, a company selling home goods through major retailers, told the BBC that the decision was made since the disputed mark was not "essential to its business". Swift's team issued a notice to the US government on 11 February with a request to block the firm's bid. Her team argued that there were similarities between the singer's trademarked designs and the company's mark, and that it could mislead consumers into thinking that Swift had endorsed the products. The BBC has contacted Swift's team for comment. Cathay Home's lawyer, Ting Geng from Geng and Associates said in a statement that the trademark application was dropped after evaluating the circumstances. "Such decisions are often practical and commercially sensible," she said. The lawyer added that the firm had previously reached a "consent-to-coexist agreement" with the singer's team regarding another registered "Swift Home" mark. Cathay Home, which has offices in North America and China, had applied for a "Swift Home" trademark for its bedding items in late 2025. In an appeal to the US Patent and Trademark Office filed last week, Swift's team said that the way Cathay Home has styled the word "Swift" in the branding closely resembled the singer's trademarked cursive mark. The mark creates a "false association" to the singer and was done with the intent of using her "goodwill and recognition" for the firm's brand, Swift's team argued. The documents said that Swift owns federal trademarks that protected the use of her name and designs on various goods, including bedding, clothes and music-related products. Swift has filed more than 300 trademarks in the US and other jurisdictions, securing her name, initials, album titles and some lyrics. The artist is estimated to have a net worth well above $1bn (Â£730 m), bolstered by her recent run of record-breaking live shows around the world.

    [Original] Vaccine Makers Curtail Research and Cut Jobs
    In a significant shift in U.S. vaccine policy, the Trump administrationâ€™s appointment of Robert F. Kennedy Jr. as health secretary has triggered widespread concern among vaccine developers and investors. Executives, including Modernaâ€™s Dr. Stephen Hoge, warn that federal policy changes are stifling innovation, with declining investment and sales across the industry. Moderna, a leader in mRNA technology, has faced severe setbacks: its mRNA flu vaccine was rejected by the FDA for flawed design, its stock has dropped over 90% since its 2021 peak, and it has canceled late-stage trials for vaccines targeting Epstein-Barr, herpes, chickenpox, and shingles. The company laid off over 800 workersâ€”10% of its workforceâ€”and lost more than $700 million in bird flu vaccine contracts. It has also shifted manufacturing to Mexico, undermining efforts to bring production back to the U.S. Smaller firms have been hit hardest. NTx Bio canceled plans to build an mRNA manufacturing plant in Plano, Texas, eliminating over 170 projected jobs, citing a â€œchallenging business climateâ€ and vaccine hesitancy. TriLink BioTechnologies and Inventprise in San Diego and Seattle, respectively, recently announced layoffs. Vaxcyte paused development of vaccines for strep and Shigella, attributing the move to shifting priorities and political uncertainty. The administration has overhauled the childhood vaccination schedule, reducing recommended immunizations from 17 to 11, with six now requiring clinician consultation. This move, described as sending â€œa chill through the entire industryâ€ by Johns Hopkins scientist Jeff Coller, has fueled concerns about declining public trust. Despite this, insurers have pledged to continue covering non-recommended vaccines through year-end, and major companies like Merck, Pfizer, Sanofi, and GSK remain financially resilient due to non-vaccine revenue streams. Merck, despite reduced Gardasil dosing recommendations, reported no sales concerns, as insurers will still cover both doses. However, broader declines in vaccination ratesâ€”particularly among kindergartnersâ€”have dampened sales. A key worry among industry leaders is whether the administration will eliminate existing liability protections for vaccine makers, which could expose them to costly lawsuits. While HHS spokesman Andrew Nixon denied anti-science intent and affirmed continued liability coverage, allies of Kennedy, including anti-vaccine activists, have signaled interest in dismantling these shields. The long-term impact on vaccine development, manufacturing, and public health remains uncertain.

    [Original] Should Drug Companies Be Advertising to Consumers?
    Tamar Abrams, 69, struggled with grief and health after losing both parents and retiring from her consulting career, moving from Arlington, Va., to Warren, R.I., where she knew only two people. As her weight reached 270 pounds and her blood pressure, cholesterol, and glucose levels rose, she sought help. Inspired by the upbeat, jingle-filled TV ads for Ozempicâ€”a GLP-1 medication for diabetesâ€”she asked her doctor if it could help her, despite not having a diabetes diagnosis. The ads, which depicted users joyfully cooking, biking, and playing pickleball, resonated with her, and she recalled humming the tune. Novo Nordisk spent $180 million in 2022 and $189 million in 2023 on direct-to-consumer advertising, with total U.S. spending on such ads reaching $201 million in 2023 and $9 billion overall, according to MediaRadar. The practice, permitted since 1997 when the FDA relaxed rules to allow TV ads with rapid risk disclosures, has expanded significantly, especially with Medicare Part D in 2006. Research shows these ads influence prescribing patterns, with patients more likely to request specific drugs, often leading to follow-up visits. However, critics argue they promote expensive, often marginally better drugs over cheaper generics. A 2023 JAMA Network Open study found nearly three-quarters of top-advertised drugs offered no significant therapeutic advantage over older ones. Dr. Aaron Kesselheim of Harvard noted that drugs without added value rely on advertising to sell. Opponents like Dr. Steven Woloshin of Dartmouth argue such ads often masquerade as education, citing disease awareness campaigns that subtly promote products. The Trump administration has pushed to reinstate pre-1997 restrictions, with HHS Secretary Robert F. Kennedy Jr. calling drug ads a â€œhook.â€ The FDA has issued 100 cease-and-desist orders and sent thousands of warnings, including one to Novo Nordisk over a misleading Wegovy ad. Meanwhile, Democratic and independent senators, including Angus King and Bernie Sanders, have introduced bills to limit or ban such ads, with Kingâ€™s proposal banning ads for the first three years post-approval and Sanders advocating a full ban. Dr. Woloshin doubts a ban will pass due to the Citizens United ruling. Dr. Kesselheim notes that pharma spends more on doctor-targeted ads than consumers, and online advertising remains largely unregulated. Abrams, now 90 pounds lighter and off blood pressure and cholesterol medication, remains conflicted about banning ads. â€œIf I hadnâ€™t asked my doctor about Ozempic, would she have suggested it?â€ she wonders. â€œOr would I still weigh 270 pounds?â€ The New Old Age is produced through a partnership with KFF Health News.

    [Original] Australian IS families in Syria camp turned back after leaving for home
    A group of 34 Australian women and children, believed to be relatives of Islamic State (IS) fighters, were released from Roj detention camp in northern Syria on Monday after nearly seven years, but returned to the camp due to â€œtechnical reasons.â€ The group, held since IS lost its last territorial stronghold in Syria in 2019, had been refused repatriation by the Australian government, which maintains it will not bring back citizens linked to IS. Australian media reported the release was halted possibly due to coordination failures among regional factions. The group had been handed over to family members who traveled to Syria and were set to travel to Damascus with a military escort, intending to proceed to Beirut to obtain passports. The Australian government reiterated it would not repatriate individuals linked to IS and warned that those who return and have committed crimes would face â€œthe full force of the law.â€ However, it acknowledged it is legally obligated to issue passports to Australian citizens who present themselves at an embassy. Roj camp houses over 2,000 people from 40 nationalities, including many women and children, with no clear path to repatriation. Among them is Shamima Begum, a British national stripped of her citizenship in 2019 over national security concerns. Camp director Hakmiyeh Ibrahim urged all countries to reclaim their citizens, stating children are growing up exposed to â€œdangerous ideas and ideologies.â€ She emphasized that the situation has become increasingly complex since families were brought to the camp in 2017. The Australian government stated its security agencies are monitoring the situation and prepared for any returnees. In 2022, a similar group of 17 Australiansâ€”four women and 13 childrenâ€”was repatriated to Sydney, sparking domestic controversy. The UK government has defended its decision to revoke Begumâ€™s citizenship, with the European Court of Human Rights calling for an investigation, though UK courts have upheld the ruling. Begum, who was 15 when she traveled from east London to IS-held territory in 2015, married an IS fighter and has since been a focal point in debates over citizenship, security, and the fate of foreign fightersâ€™ families.

    [Original] Navalny's mother says she always knew he was murdered after poison finding
    Lyudmila Navalnaya, mother of the late Russian opposition leader Alexei Navalny, said the UK and European alliesâ€™ assessment that her son was killed with a toxin derived from a dart frog confirms her belief he was murdered.     [Original] Ancient bone may prove legendary war elephant crossing of Alps
    ShareSaveGeorgina RannardScience reporterShareSaveGetty ImagesHannibal's invasion of Europe has acquired almost mythical statusAn elephant foot bone found by archaeologists digging in southern Spain may be evidence that a troop of war elephants stomped through ancient Europe. It would be the first concrete proof of the legendary Carthaginian General Hannibal's troop of battle elephants, according to academics. Drawings of Hannibal's war against the Romans had long suggested that the beasts were used in fighting, but no hard evidence backed up the theories. Now the creatures' skeletal remains appear to have been found in an Iron Age dig near Cordoba. "Beyond ivory, the discovery of elephant remains in European archaeological contexts is exceptionally rare," says the team of scientists in a paper published in Journal of Archaeological Science: Reports. Often considered one of the most successful commanders of classical times, Hannibal led his army from the powerful imperial city Carthage, in modern day Tunisia, into Europe as he battled to control the Mediterranean. It is thought he took soldiers and animals from Carthage through Spain and France to invade Italy, crossing the Alps with 37 elephants in 218 BCE during the second of the so-called Punic Wars. The remains found in Spain are presumed to be from an animal that died before reaching the Alps. The archaeologists, led by Professor Rafael M. MartÃ­nez SÃ¡nchez, found the elephant's bone beneath a collapsed wall on a site called Colina de los Quemados. They used carbon dating techniques to estimate the age of the 10cm cube-shaped bone. The result led them to believe it is from the Second Punic War. They also compared the bone of modern elephants and steppe mammoths to determine which animal it came from. The team found artillery, coins and ceramics during the excavations in 2020, providing more clues that the place was the site of a battle. "As non-native species and the largest living terrestrial animals, these imported beasts would have required transportation by ship," the academics said. They say that it is very unlikely that dead animals were transported, and the bones are unattractive suggesting they were not decorative or used in craft. But the scientists say it will be very challenging to work out which species of elephant the creature was. "While [the bone] would not represent one of the mythical specimens Hannibal took across the Alps, it could potentially embody the first known relic âˆ’ so sought after by European scholars of the Modern Age âˆ’ of the animals used in the Punic Roman wars for the control of the Mediterranean," the scientists conclude in their paper.

    [Original] Pressure grows for release of remaining Venezuelan political prisoners
    ShareSaveVanessa BuschschlÃ¼terShareSaveMiguel Gutierrez/EPA/ShutterstockMany of those protesting outside the Zona 7 detention centre are wearing T-shirts reading "free all the political prisoners" and carrying pictures of their jailed relativesPressure is growing on the Venezuelan interim government to free the remaining political prisoners being held in the country. A pressure group campaigning on detainees' behalf, Foro Penal, said on Sunday that it had been able to verify that 444 political prisoners had been released since US forces seized Venezuelan leader NicolÃ¡s Maduro last month. But hundreds more remain behind bars, according to a tally kept by Foro Penal, even though the Trump administration has pressured Venezuelan officials to speed up their release. Activists say the health of some relatives who have gone on hunger strike to demand the release of their loved ones is quickly deteriorating. Opposition party Vente Venezuela posted footage of one of the relatives - who appears to be unconscious - being tended to by others outside the Zona 7 detention centre in the capital, Caracas. According to a pressure group, 10 people went on hunger strike on Saturday to demand that their relatives, who are being held at Zona 7, be freed. They have been holding a vigil outside the police detention centre since 8 January, when the head of Venezuela's National Assembly, Jorge RodrÃ­guez, first announced that "a significant number" of prisoners would be freed as a goodwill gesture to demonstrate what he said was the interim government's will "seek peace". While the Trump administration has praised Venezuela's interim government for the progress it has made so far, activists within the South American country have denounced delays and a lack of transparency in who is being freed. Members of the opposition have also denounced that those who have been released have been barred from speaking out publicly. They cite the case of opposition politician Juan Pablo Guanipa as evidence that those released are not "really free". Guanipa was released on 8 February after eight months in jail, only to be seized again hours later for allegedly breaking the conditions of his release. He has since been placed under house arrest. Last week, students took to the streets to demand an amnesty for those who have been jailed as part of the widespread repression against critics of the government of NicolÃ¡s Maduro. Venezuela's interim government - led by Maduro's former vice-president, Delcy RodrÃ­guez - has put forward an amnesty bill since Maduro was removed from power in a US raid on 3 January. The bill passed its first reading unanimously on 5 February but lawmakers failed to approve it as scheduled last week as they could not agree on how it should be applied. The bill is due to be discussed further this week.

    [Original] 'It's never too late': Savannah Guthrie's new plea for mother's release as FBI analyses glove
    US television anchor Savannah Guthrie has issued a public plea for the release of her 84-year-old mother, Nancy Guthrie, two weeks after she vanished in a suspected kidnapping. In a video appeal, Guthrie urged anyone holding her mother to â€œdo the right thing,â€ stating, â€œIt is never too late.â€ Authorities believe Nancy was abducted in the early hours of February 1, based on doorbell-camera footage showing a masked man wearing gloves and a backpack. Of approximately 16 gloves collected near her residence, most were discarded by searchers; this one is distinct and under further analysis. The FBI is awaiting quality control confirmation before entering an unknown male DNA profile into its database. The suspect, described as 5ft 9in to 5ft 10in (175â€“177cm) with an average build, was seen attempting to obstruct the camera by covering it with a gloved hand and using brush from the yard to block the view. Her church community noticed her absence the next day and alerted her family. A search warrant was executed at a residence two miles from her home on Friday, but no arrests were made. DNA found at her address does not belong to her or close associates. The FBI has doubled the reward for information to $100,000. Officials stress the suspect description remains a key focus, though no possibilities are being ruled out.

    [Original] Chinaâ€™s green energy leader Inner Mongolia starts work on new â€˜flexibleâ€™ power plants
    The two plants will use coal as a backup to renewable sources such as wind and solar power in a major strategic shift The Chinese region of Inner Mongolia expects to start work this year on two new â€œflexibleâ€ power plants mixing coal and green energy.The plants, which will provide power to the eastern economic hubs of Shanghai and Jiangsu province, mark a major strategic shift in the use of coal because the fossil fuel will now be used to plug gaps in the power generated by wind and solar energy rather than being the primary source of energy. The autonomous regionâ€™s energy bureau said the National Energy Administration had approved plans for two power and transmission plants in the Kubuqi Desert. The Shanghai plant will have the capacity to supply 8 gigawatts of solar power, 4.5 kilowatts of wind power, 2.6GW of supporting coal-fired power and 9 gigawatt-hours of new energy storage. The Jiangsu plant is of a similar scale, although it has a slightly smaller wind and energy storage capacity, the regional authorities said.

    [Original] UBS China venture faces wrath of silver investors after valuation change increases losses
    More than 200,000 people file complaints as UBS SDICâ€™s attempted remedy for sudden change of fundâ€™s price benchmark falls flat with some Thousands of Chinese retail investors spent the last day of the Year of the Snake on Monday filing complaints against UBS SDIC Fund Management for an overnight valuation change that caused steep losses for holders of its silver fund, even after the firm offered remedies. More than 200,000 people lodged complaints against the operator of the countryâ€™s only silver fund after the controversial change on February 2, according to Xiaofei Bao, an online Chinese consumer protection platform. The switch came amid a silver crash on January 30, when global prices plunged more than 30 per cent from around US$121 per ounce. Shanghai futures contracts, capped by a 17 per cent daily price limit, could not keep pace with the rout on overseas exchanges, expanding the gap between domestic and international prices. After trading closed on February 2, UBS SDIC, a joint venture between Swiss bank UBS and a unit of state-owned SDIC Group, switched the valuation benchmark for its listed open-ended fund (LOF), called UBS SDIC Silver Futures Fund LOF, from Shanghai Futures Exchange settlement prices to international market prices without prior notice. The move turned what investors thought would be a loss capped at about 17 per cent into a 31.5 per cent drop. After setting up a task force on February 6 to mitigate the impact of the revaluation on retail investors, the fund manager unveiled a tiered compensation plan for investors who redeemed on February 2.
